0 0 0);-webkit-clip-path:inset(50%);clip-path:inset(50%);overflow:hidden;margin:-1px;}</style><a href="#main-content" rel="noreferrer" class="euiSkipLink 1km4ln8-euiButtonDisplayContent">.css-1km4ln8-euiButtonDisplayContent{block-size:100%;inline-size:100%;display:-webkit-box;display:-webkit-flex;display:-ms-flexbox;display:flex;-webkit-box-pack:center;-ms-flex-pack:center;-webkit-justify-content:center;justify-content:center;-webkit-align-items:center;-webkit-box-align:center;-ms-flex-align:center;align-items:center;vertical-align:middle;gap:8px;}</style><span class="css-1km4ln8-euiButtonDisplayContent"><span class="eui-textTruncate">Skip to main content</span></span></a></div><header class="primary header-wrapper render-oldNav"></header><main id="main-content" role="main"><div class="jsx-3535804128 blog-detail"><section class="jsx-3535804128"><div style="background-image:linear-gradient(rgba(0, 85, 113, 0.5), rgba(0, 85, 113, 0.5)), url(https://static-www.elastic.co/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt3449a8b2e94a5f02/5eea6d715f3aa42c4669df76/blog-banner-security-radar-anomaly-monitor.png);background-repeat:no-repeat;background-position:center;background-size:cover" class="jsx-3535804128 hero-image py-8"><div class="jsx-3535804128 container"><div class="jsx-3535804128 full-bleed-image-wrapper"><div class="jsx-3535804128 full-bleed-image"><span class="jsx-3535804128 blog-date"><time dateTime="1615910400000">March 16, 2021</time></span><a class="category-label label-product" href="/blog/category/product">Product</a><div class="title-wrapper"><h1 tag="H1" class="h3 color-variant-white" id="detecting-cobalt-strike-with-memory-signatures">Detecting Cobalt Strike with memory signatures</h1></div><div class="jsx-3535804128 author-name d-flex my-2 paragraph-medium"><span class="jsx-3535804128 mr-1">By</span><div class="jsx-3535804128 d-flex flex-wrap"><a href="/blog/author/joe-desimone">Joe Desimone</a></div></div><p class="jsx-3535804128 share-label mb-1">Share</p><ul class="jsx-3582162638 horizontalLayout social-media"><li title="Share on Twitter" class="jsx-3582162638 social-btn twitter"><a href="#" alt="Share on Twitter"></a><span class="jsx-3582162638 sr-only">Share on Twitter</span></li><li title="Share on Facebook" class="jsx-3582162638 social-btn facebook"><a href="#" alt="Share on Facebook"></a><span class="jsx-3582162638 sr-only">Share on Facebook</span></li><li title="Share on LinkedIn" class="jsx-3582162638 social-btn linkedin"><a href="#" alt="Share on LinkedIn"></a><span class="jsx-3582162638 sr-only">Share on LinkedInr</span></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="layout layout-05"></div><div class="jsx-3535804128 article-post-wrapper blog clearfix paragraph-medium"><div class="jsx-3535804128 container"><div class="jsx-3535804128 row"><div class="jsx-3535804128 col-12 col-lg-9"><div class="jsx-3535804128 disclaimer-wrapper mb-6 p-3"><p><span style="color:rgb(29, 28, 29);font-size:12pt">This blog discusses, mentions, or contains links to an Elastic training program that is now retired. For more Elastic resources, please visit the </span><a href="https://www.elastic.co/getting-started"><span style="font-size:12pt">Getting Started page</span></a><span style="color:rgb(29, 28, 29);font-size:12pt">.</span></p></div><div class="jsx-3535804128 paragraph-medium"><p>At Elastic Security, we approach the challenge of threat detection with various methods. Traditionally, we have focused on machine learning models and behaviors. These two methods are powerful because they can detect never-before-seen malware. Historically, we’ve felt that signatures are too easily evaded, but we also recognize that ease of evasion is only one of many factors to consider. Performance and false positive rates are also critical in measuring a detection technique&#x27;s effectiveness.</p><p>Signatures, while unable to detect unknown malware, have false positive rates that approach zero and have associated labels that help prioritize alerts. For example, an alert for TrickBot or REvil Ransomware requires more immediate action than a potentially unwanted adware variant. Even if we could hypothetically catch only half of known malware with signatures, that is still a huge win when layered with other protections, considering the other benefits. Realistically we can do even better.</p><p>One roadblock to creating signatures that provide long-term value is the widespread use of packers and throw-away malware loaders. These components rapidly evolve to evade signature detection; however, the final malware payload is eventually decrypted and executed in memory.</p><p>To step around the issue of packers and loaders, we can focus signature detection strategies on in-memory content. This effectively extends the shelf life of the signature from days to months. In this post, we will use Cobalt Strike as an example for leveraging in-memory signatures. </p><h2><strong>Signaturing Cobalt Strike</strong></h2><p><a href="https://www.cobaltstrike.com/">Cobalt Strike</a> is a popular framework for conducting red team operations and adversary simulation. Presumably due to its ease of use, stability, and stealth features, it is also a favorite tool for <a href="https://www.fireeye.com/blog/threat-research/2017/05/cyber-espionage-apt32.html">bad actors</a> with even more <a href="https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/sodinokibi-ransomware-cobalt-strike-pos">nefarious</a> intentions. There have been various techniques for detecting Beacon, Cobalt Strike’s endpoint payload. This includes looking for <a href="https://www.elastic.co/blog/hunting-memory">unbacked threads</a>, and, more recently, built-in <a href="https://labs.f-secure.com/blog/detecting-cobalt-strike-default-modules-via-named-pipe-analysis/">named pipes</a>. However, due to the level of <a href="https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2019/05/02/cobalt-strike-3-14-post-ex-omakase-shimasu/">configurability</a> in Beacon, there are usually ways to evade public detection strategies. Here we will attempt to use memory signatures as an alternative detection strategy.</p><p>Beacon is typically <a href="https://github.com/stephenfewer/ReflectiveDLLInjection">reflectively loaded</a> into memory and never touches disk in a directly signaturable form. Further, Beacon can be configured with a variety of in-memory obfuscation options to hide its payload. For example, the <a href="https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2018/09/06/cobalt-strike-3-12-blink-and-youll-miss-it/">obfuscate-and-sleep</a> option attempts to mask portions of the Beacon payload between callbacks to specifically evade signature-based memory scans. We will need to consider this option when developing signatures, but it is still easy to signature Beacon even with these advanced stealth features.</p><h2><strong>Diving in</strong></h2><p>We will start by obtaining a handful of Beacon payloads with the <a href="https://github.com/rsmudge/Malleable-C2-Profiles/blob/master/normal/reference.profile#L254-L255"><strong>sleep_mask</strong></a> option enabled and disabled with the most recent releases (hashes in reference section). Starting with a sample with <strong>sleep_mask</strong> disabled, after detonation we can locate Beacon in memory with Process Hacker by looking for a thread which calls SleepEx from an unbacked region:</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt7b127a57723668a4/603e6e90982f2a0bdaf5a89b/1-process-hacker-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blt7b127a57723668a4" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/></p><p>From there, we can save the associated memory region to disk for analysis:</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt8fd59d9d51f487e3/603e6e9ef9638443346d3da1/2-memory-region-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blt8fd59d9d51f487e3" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/></p><p>The easiest win would be to pick a few unique strings from this region and use those as our signature. To demonstrate, will will be writing signatures with <a href="https://virustotal.github.io/yara/">yara</a>, an industry standard tool for this purpose:</p><pre class="prettyprint">rule cobaltstrike_beacon_strings<br/>{<br/>meta:<br/>    author = &quot;Elastic&quot;<br/>    description = &quot;Identifies strings used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL.&quot;<br/>strings:<br/>    $a = &quot;%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d&quot;<br/>    $b = &quot;Started service %s on %s&quot;<br/>    $c = &quot;%s as %s\\%s: %d&quot;<br/>condition:<br/>    2 of them<br/>}<br/></pre><p>This would give us a good base of coverage, but we can do better by looking at the samples with <strong>sleep_mask</strong>enabled. If we look in memory where the MZ/PE header would normally be found, we now see it is obfuscated:</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt68be7567c87b8f15/603e6eb71322a9094ddecf50/3-obfuscated-header-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blt68be7567c87b8f15" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/></p><p>Quickly looking at this, we can see a lot of repeated bytes (0x80 in this case) where we would actually expect null bytes. This can be an indication that Beacon is using a simple one-byte XOR obfuscation. To confirm, we can use <a href="https://gchq.github.io/CyberChef/">CyberChef</a>:</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt51fc44c10e0289c9/603e6ec708636f3d7749a259/4-cyberchef-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blt51fc44c10e0289c9" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/></p><p>As you can see, the “This program cannot be run in DOS mode” string appears after decoding, confirming our theory. Because a single byte XOR is one of the oldest tricks in the book, yara actually supports native detection with the <a href="https://yara.readthedocs.io/en/stable/writingrules.html"><strong>xor</strong></a> modifier:</p><pre class="prettyprint">rule cobaltstrike_beacon_xor_strings<br/>{<br/>meta:<br/>    author = &quot;Elastic&quot;<br/>    description = &quot;Identifies XOR&#x27;d strings used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL.&quot;<br/>strings:<br/>    $a = &quot;%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d&quot; xor(0x01-0xff)<br/>    $b = &quot;Started service %s on %s&quot; xor(0x01-0xff)<br/>    $c = &quot;%s as %s\\%s: %d&quot; xor(0x01-0xff)<br/>condition:<br/>    2 of them<br/>}<br/></pre><p>We can confirm detection for our yara rules thus far by providing a PID while scanning:</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blte78d10de1484f20e/603e6ed5f9638443346d3da5/5-confirming-detection-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blte78d10de1484f20e" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/></p><p>However, we are not quite done. After testing this signature on a sample with the latest version of Beacon (4.2 as of this writing), the obfuscation routine has been improved. The routine can be located by following the call stack as shown earlier. It now uses a 13-byte XOR key as shown in the following IDA Pro snippet:</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6ca50e04e513814b/603e6efbacf0d53d70c5accc/6-ida-pro-snippet-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blt6ca50e04e513814b" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/><br/></p><p>Fortunately, Beacon’s obfuscate-and-sleep option only obfuscates strings and data, leaving the entire code section ripe for signaturing. There is the question of which function in the code section we should develop a signature for, but that is worth its own blog post. For now, we can just create a signature on the deobfuscation routine, which should work well:</p><pre class="prettyprint">rule cobaltstrike_beacon_4_2_decrypt<br/>{<br/>meta:<br/>    author = &quot;Elastic&quot;<br/>    description = &quot;Identifies deobfuscation routine used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL version 4.2.&quot;<br/>strings:<br/>    $a_x64 = {4C 8B 53 08 45 8B 0A 45 8B 5A 04 4D 8D 52 08 45 85 C9 75 05 45 85 DB 74 33 45 3B CB 73 E6 49 8B F9 4C 8B 03}<br/>    $a_x86 = {8B 46 04 8B 08 8B 50 04 83 C0 08 89 55 08 89 45 0C 85 C9 75 04 85 D2 74 23 3B CA 73 E6 8B 06 8D 3C 08 33 D2}<br/>condition:<br/>     any of them<br/>}<br/></pre><p>We can validate that we can detect Beacon even while it is in its stealthy sleep state (both 32- and 64-bit variants):</p><p><img src="https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt70a33ec8ee2d14c1/603e6eef7d801145b7fb6fd5/7-detection-beacon-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png" data-sys-asset-uid="blt70a33ec8ee2d14c1" alt="" style="display:block;margin:auto"/></p><p>To build this into a more robust detection, we could regularly scan all processes on the system (or entire enterprise). This could be done with the following powershell one-liner:</p><pre class="prettyprint">powershell -command &quot;Get-Process | ForEach-Object {c:\yara64.exe my_rules.yar $_.ID}&quot;<br/></pre><h2><strong>Wrapping up</strong></h2><p>Signature-based detection, while often looked down upon, is a valuable detection strategy — especially when we consider in-memory scanning. With only a handful of signatures, we can detect Cobalt Strike regardless of configuration or stealth features enabled with an effective false positive rate of zero.</p><h3>Reference hashes</h3><pre class="prettyprint">7d2c09a06d731a56bca7af2f5d3badef53624f025d77ababe6a14be28540a17a<br/>277c2a0a18d7dc04993b6dc7ce873a086ab267391a9acbbc4a140e9c4658372a<br/>A0788b85266fedd64dab834cb605a31b81fd11a3439dc3a6370bb34e512220e2<br/>2db56e74f43b1a826beff9b577933135791ee44d8e66fa111b9b2af32948235c<br/>3d65d80b1eb8626cf327c046db0c20ba4ed1b588b8c2f1286bc09b8f4da204f2<br/></pre><h2>Learn more about Elastic Security</h2><p>Familiarize yourself (if you haven&#x27;t already) with the powerful protection, detection, and response capabilities of Elastic Agent. Start your <a href="https://cloud.elastic.co/registration?elektra=en-security-page">free 14-day trial</a> (no credit card required) or <a href="https://www.elastic.co/downloads/">download our products</a>, free, for your on-prem deployment. And take advantage of our <a href="https://www.elastic.co/training/elastic-security-quick-start">Quick Start training</a> to set you up for success.</p></div><div class="layout layout-07"></div></div></div></div></div><div class="layout layout-07"></div></section></div></main><div id="elastic-footer"></div><footer class="Footer_footer__OYoYH" aria-label="footer"><div All other brand names, product names, or trademarks belong to their respective owners.</p><style>[class*="Patterns_patterns__"], [class*="Hero_heroContent__"] .pill { display: none !important; }</style></div></div></div></div></div></footer></div><script id="__NEXT_DATA__" type="application/json">{"props":{"pageProps":{"entry":[{"uid":"bltc89fa5d4435a3727","_version":14,"locale":"en-us","ACL":{},"abstract_l10n":"Signature-based detection — especially in-memory scanning — can be a valuable detection strategy. In this blog, learn how to detect Cobalt Strike regardless of configuration or stealth features enabled with an effective false positive rate of zero.","author":[{"locale":"en-us","ACL":{},"_version":1,"company_l10n":"","created_at":"2019-12-20T16:44:46.882Z","created_by":"blt368f4800fe5bde87","email_id":"","facebook_id":"","first_name":"Joe","image":null,"job_title_l10n":"","key":"","last_name":"Desimone","paragraph_l10n":"","subtitle_l10n":"","tags":[],"title":"Joe Desimone","uid":"blt880f6b244651c966","updated_at":"2019-12-20T16:44:46.882Z","updated_by":"blt368f4800fe5bde87","publish_details":{"environment":"blt835e2c3b7e9eb7fb","locale":"en-us","time":"2020-02-03T08:00:00.000Z","user":"blt3044324473ef223b70bc674c"}}],"body_l10n":"\u003cp\u003eAt Elastic Security, we approach the challenge of threat detection with various methods. Traditionally, we have focused on machine learning models and behaviors. These two methods are powerful because they can detect never-before-seen malware. Historically, we’ve felt that signatures are too easily evaded, but we also recognize that ease of evasion is only one of many factors to consider. Performance and false positive rates are also critical in measuring a detection technique's effectiveness.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSignatures, while unable to detect unknown malware, have false positive rates that approach zero and have associated labels that help prioritize alerts. For example, an alert for TrickBot or REvil Ransomware requires more immediate action than a potentially unwanted adware variant. Even if we could hypothetically catch only half of known malware with signatures, that is still a huge win when layered with other protections, considering the other benefits. Realistically we can do even better.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOne roadblock to creating signatures that provide long-term value is the widespread use of packers and throw-away malware loaders. These components rapidly evolve to evade signature detection; however, the final malware payload is eventually decrypted and executed in memory.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo step around the issue of packers and loaders, we can focus signature detection strategies on in-memory content. This effectively extends the shelf life of the signature from days to months. In this post, we will use Cobalt Strike as an example for leveraging in-memory signatures.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSignaturing Cobalt Strike\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/\"\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/a\u003e is a popular framework for conducting red team operations and adversary simulation. Presumably due to its ease of use, stability, and stealth features, it is also a favorite tool for \u003ca href=\"https://www.fireeye.com/blog/threat-research/2017/05/cyber-espionage-apt32.html\"\u003ebad actors\u003c/a\u003e with even more \u003ca href=\"https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/sodinokibi-ransomware-cobalt-strike-pos\"\u003enefarious\u003c/a\u003e intentions. There have been various techniques for detecting Beacon, Cobalt Strike’s endpoint payload. This includes looking for \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/hunting-memory\"\u003eunbacked threads\u003c/a\u003e, and, more recently, built-in \u003ca href=\"https://labs.f-secure.com/blog/detecting-cobalt-strike-default-modules-via-named-pipe-analysis/\"\u003enamed pipes\u003c/a\u003e. However, due to the level of \u003ca href=\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2019/05/02/cobalt-strike-3-14-post-ex-omakase-shimasu/\"\u003econfigurability\u003c/a\u003e in Beacon, there are usually ways to evade public detection strategies. Here we will attempt to use memory signatures as an alternative detection strategy.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBeacon is typically \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/stephenfewer/ReflectiveDLLInjection\"\u003ereflectively loaded\u003c/a\u003e into memory and never touches disk in a directly signaturable form. Further, Beacon can be configured with a variety of in-memory obfuscation options to hide its payload. For example, the \u003ca href=\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2018/09/06/cobalt-strike-3-12-blink-and-youll-miss-it/\"\u003eobfuscate-and-sleep\u003c/a\u003e option attempts to mask portions of the Beacon payload between callbacks to specifically evade signature-based memory scans. We will need to consider this option when developing signatures, but it is still easy to signature Beacon even with these advanced stealth features.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDiving in\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe will start by obtaining a handful of Beacon payloads with the \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/rsmudge/Malleable-C2-Profiles/blob/master/normal/reference.profile#L254-L255\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003esleep_mask\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e option enabled and disabled with the most recent releases (hashes in reference section). Starting with a sample with \u003cstrong\u003esleep_mask\u003c/strong\u003e disabled, after detonation we can locate Beacon in memory with Process Hacker by looking for a thread which calls SleepEx from an unbacked region:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt7b127a57723668a4/603e6e90982f2a0bdaf5a89b/1-process-hacker-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7b127a57723668a4\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom there, we can save the associated memory region to disk for analysis:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt8fd59d9d51f487e3/603e6e9ef9638443346d3da1/2-memory-region-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt8fd59d9d51f487e3\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe easiest win would be to pick a few unique strings from this region and use those as our signature. To demonstrate, will will be writing signatures with \u003ca href=\"https://virustotal.github.io/yara/\"\u003eyara\u003c/a\u003e, an industry standard tool for this purpose:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003erule cobaltstrike_beacon_strings\u003cbr /\u003e{\u003cbr /\u003emeta:\u003cbr /\u003e    author = \"Elastic\"\u003cbr /\u003e    description = \"Identifies strings used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL.\"\u003cbr /\u003estrings:\u003cbr /\u003e    $a = \"%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d\"\u003cbr /\u003e    $b = \"Started service %s on %s\"\u003cbr /\u003e    $c = \"%s as %s\\\\%s: %d\"\u003cbr /\u003econdition:\u003cbr /\u003e    2 of them\u003cbr /\u003e}\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis would give us a good base of coverage, but we can do better by looking at the samples with \u003cstrong\u003esleep_mask\u003c/strong\u003eenabled. If we look in memory where the MZ/PE header would normally be found, we now see it is obfuscated:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt68be7567c87b8f15/603e6eb71322a9094ddecf50/3-obfuscated-header-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt68be7567c87b8f15\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eQuickly looking at this, we can see a lot of repeated bytes (0x80 in this case) where we would actually expect null bytes. This can be an indication that Beacon is using a simple one-byte XOR obfuscation. To confirm, we can use \u003ca href=\"https://gchq.github.io/CyberChef/\"\u003eCyberChef\u003c/a\u003e:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt51fc44c10e0289c9/603e6ec708636f3d7749a259/4-cyberchef-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt51fc44c10e0289c9\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs you can see, the “This program cannot be run in DOS mode” string appears after decoding, confirming our theory. Because a single byte XOR is one of the oldest tricks in the book, yara actually supports native detection with the \u003ca href=\"https://yara.readthedocs.io/en/stable/writingrules.html\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003exor\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e modifier:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003erule cobaltstrike_beacon_xor_strings\u003cbr /\u003e{\u003cbr /\u003emeta:\u003cbr /\u003e    author = \"Elastic\"\u003cbr /\u003e    description = \"Identifies XOR'd strings used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL.\"\u003cbr /\u003estrings:\u003cbr /\u003e    $a = \"%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d\" xor(0x01-0xff)\u003cbr /\u003e    $b = \"Started service %s on %s\" xor(0x01-0xff)\u003cbr /\u003e    $c = \"%s as %s\\\\%s: %d\" xor(0x01-0xff)\u003cbr /\u003econdition:\u003cbr /\u003e    2 of them\u003cbr /\u003e}\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe can confirm detection for our yara rules thus far by providing a PID while scanning:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blte78d10de1484f20e/603e6ed5f9638443346d3da5/5-confirming-detection-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blte78d10de1484f20e\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eHowever, we are not quite done. After testing this signature on a sample with the latest version of Beacon (4.2 as of this writing), the obfuscation routine has been improved. The routine can be located by following the call stack as shown earlier. It now uses a 13-byte XOR key as shown in the following IDA Pro snippet:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6ca50e04e513814b/603e6efbacf0d53d70c5accc/6-ida-pro-snippet-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt6ca50e04e513814b\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFortunately, Beacon’s obfuscate-and-sleep option only obfuscates strings and data, leaving the entire code section ripe for signaturing. There is the question of which function in the code section we should develop a signature for, but that is worth its own blog post. For now, we can just create a signature on the deobfuscation routine, which should work well:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003erule cobaltstrike_beacon_4_2_decrypt\u003cbr /\u003e{\u003cbr /\u003emeta:\u003cbr /\u003e    author = \"Elastic\"\u003cbr /\u003e    description = \"Identifies deobfuscation routine used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL version 4.2.\"\u003cbr /\u003estrings:\u003cbr /\u003e    $a_x64 = {4C 8B 53 08 45 8B 0A 45 8B 5A 04 4D 8D 52 08 45 85 C9 75 05 45 85 DB 74 33 45 3B CB 73 E6 49 8B F9 4C 8B 03}\u003cbr /\u003e    $a_x86 = {8B 46 04 8B 08 8B 50 04 83 C0 08 89 55 08 89 45 0C 85 C9 75 04 85 D2 74 23 3B CA 73 E6 8B 06 8D 3C 08 33 D2}\u003cbr /\u003econdition:\u003cbr /\u003e     any of them\u003cbr /\u003e}\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe can validate that we can detect Beacon even while it is in its stealthy sleep state (both 32- and 64-bit variants):\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt70a33ec8ee2d14c1/603e6eef7d801145b7fb6fd5/7-detection-beacon-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt70a33ec8ee2d14c1\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo build this into a more robust detection, we could regularly scan all processes on the system (or entire enterprise). This could be done with the following powershell one-liner:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003epowershell -command \"Get-Process | ForEach-Object {c:\\yara64.exe my_rules.yar $_.ID}\"\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWrapping up\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eSignature-based detection, while often looked down upon, is a valuable detection strategy — especially when we consider in-memory scanning. With only a handful of signatures, we can detect Cobalt Strike regardless of configuration or stealth features enabled with an effective false positive rate of zero.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eReference hashes\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003e7d2c09a06d731a56bca7af2f5d3badef53624f025d77ababe6a14be28540a17a\u003cbr /\u003e277c2a0a18d7dc04993b6dc7ce873a086ab267391a9acbbc4a140e9c4658372a\u003cbr /\u003eA0788b85266fedd64dab834cb605a31b81fd11a3439dc3a6370bb34e512220e2\u003cbr /\u003e2db56e74f43b1a826beff9b577933135791ee44d8e66fa111b9b2af32948235c\u003cbr /\u003e3d65d80b1eb8626cf327c046db0c20ba4ed1b588b8c2f1286bc09b8f4da204f2\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003ch2\u003eLearn more about Elastic Security\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFamiliarize yourself (if you haven't already) with the powerful protection, detection, and response capabilities of Elastic Agent. \u0026 compliance","tags":[],"title":"Security \u0026 Cobalt Strike with memory signatures","title_l10n":"Detecting Cobalt Strike with memory signatures","updated_at":"2025-06-27T15:40:29.669Z","updated_by":"blt3e52848e0cb3c394","url":"/blog/detecting-cobalt-strike-with-memory-signatures","publish_details":{"time":"2025-06-27T15:40:33.371Z","user":"blt3e52848e0cb3c394","environment":"blt835e2c3b7e9eb7fb","locale":"en-us"}}],"locale":"en-us","enData":[{"uid":"bltc89fa5d4435a3727","_version":14,"locale":"en-us","ACL":{},"abstract_l10n":"Signature-based detection — especially in-memory scanning — can be a valuable detection strategy. In this blog, learn how to detect Cobalt Strike regardless of configuration or stealth features enabled with an effective false positive rate of zero.","author":["blt880f6b244651c966"],"body_l10n":"\u003cp\u003eAt Elastic Security, we approach the challenge of threat detection with various methods. Traditionally, we have focused on machine learning models and behaviors. These two methods are powerful because they can detect never-before-seen malware. Historically, we’ve felt that signatures are too easily evaded, but we also recognize that ease of evasion is only one of many factors to consider. Performance and false positive rates are also critical in measuring a detection technique's effectiveness.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSignatures, while unable to detect unknown malware, have false positive rates that approach zero and have associated labels that help prioritize alerts. For example, an alert for TrickBot or REvil Ransomware requires more immediate action than a potentially unwanted adware variant. Even if we could hypothetically catch only half of known malware with signatures, that is still a huge win when layered with other protections, considering the other benefits. Realistically we can do even better.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOne roadblock to creating signatures that provide long-term value is the widespread use of packers and throw-away malware loaders. These components rapidly evolve to evade signature detection; however, the final malware payload is eventually decrypted and executed in memory.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo step around the issue of packers and loaders, we can focus signature detection strategies on in-memory content. This effectively extends the shelf life of the signature from days to months. In this post, we will use Cobalt Strike as an example for leveraging in-memory signatures.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSignaturing Cobalt Strike\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://www.cobaltstrike.com/\"\u003eCobalt Strike\u003c/a\u003e is a popular framework for conducting red team operations and adversary simulation. Presumably due to its ease of use, stability, and stealth features, it is also a favorite tool for \u003ca href=\"https://www.fireeye.com/blog/threat-research/2017/05/cyber-espionage-apt32.html\"\u003ebad actors\u003c/a\u003e with even more \u003ca href=\"https://symantec-enterprise-blogs.security.com/blogs/threat-intelligence/sodinokibi-ransomware-cobalt-strike-pos\"\u003enefarious\u003c/a\u003e intentions. There have been various techniques for detecting Beacon, Cobalt Strike’s endpoint payload. This includes looking for \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/blog/hunting-memory\"\u003eunbacked threads\u003c/a\u003e, and, more recently, built-in \u003ca href=\"https://labs.f-secure.com/blog/detecting-cobalt-strike-default-modules-via-named-pipe-analysis/\"\u003enamed pipes\u003c/a\u003e. However, due to the level of \u003ca href=\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2019/05/02/cobalt-strike-3-14-post-ex-omakase-shimasu/\"\u003econfigurability\u003c/a\u003e in Beacon, there are usually ways to evade public detection strategies. Here we will attempt to use memory signatures as an alternative detection strategy.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eBeacon is typically \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/stephenfewer/ReflectiveDLLInjection\"\u003ereflectively loaded\u003c/a\u003e into memory and never touches disk in a directly signaturable form. Further, Beacon can be configured with a variety of in-memory obfuscation options to hide its payload. For example, the \u003ca href=\"https://blog.cobaltstrike.com/2018/09/06/cobalt-strike-3-12-blink-and-youll-miss-it/\"\u003eobfuscate-and-sleep\u003c/a\u003e option attempts to mask portions of the Beacon payload between callbacks to specifically evade signature-based memory scans. We will need to consider this option when developing signatures, but it is still easy to signature Beacon even with these advanced stealth features.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eDiving in\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe will start by obtaining a handful of Beacon payloads with the \u003ca href=\"https://github.com/rsmudge/Malleable-C2-Profiles/blob/master/normal/reference.profile#L254-L255\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003esleep_mask\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e option enabled and disabled with the most recent releases (hashes in reference section). Starting with a sample with \u003cstrong\u003esleep_mask\u003c/strong\u003e disabled, after detonation we can locate Beacon in memory with Process Hacker by looking for a thread which calls SleepEx from an unbacked region:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt7b127a57723668a4/603e6e90982f2a0bdaf5a89b/1-process-hacker-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt7b127a57723668a4\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFrom there, we can save the associated memory region to disk for analysis:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt8fd59d9d51f487e3/603e6e9ef9638443346d3da1/2-memory-region-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt8fd59d9d51f487e3\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe easiest win would be to pick a few unique strings from this region and use those as our signature. To demonstrate, will will be writing signatures with \u003ca href=\"https://virustotal.github.io/yara/\"\u003eyara\u003c/a\u003e, an industry standard tool for this purpose:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003erule cobaltstrike_beacon_strings\u003cbr /\u003e{\u003cbr /\u003emeta:\u003cbr /\u003e    author = \"Elastic\"\u003cbr /\u003e    description = \"Identifies strings used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL.\"\u003cbr /\u003estrings:\u003cbr /\u003e    $a = \"%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d\"\u003cbr /\u003e    $b = \"Started service %s on %s\"\u003cbr /\u003e    $c = \"%s as %s\\\\%s: %d\"\u003cbr /\u003econdition:\u003cbr /\u003e    2 of them\u003cbr /\u003e}\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis would give us a good base of coverage, but we can do better by looking at the samples with \u003cstrong\u003esleep_mask\u003c/strong\u003eenabled. If we look in memory where the MZ/PE header would normally be found, we now see it is obfuscated:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt68be7567c87b8f15/603e6eb71322a9094ddecf50/3-obfuscated-header-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt68be7567c87b8f15\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eQuickly looking at this, we can see a lot of repeated bytes (0x80 in this case) where we would actually expect null bytes. This can be an indication that Beacon is using a simple one-byte XOR obfuscation. To confirm, we can use \u003ca href=\"https://gchq.github.io/CyberChef/\"\u003eCyberChef\u003c/a\u003e:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt51fc44c10e0289c9/603e6ec708636f3d7749a259/4-cyberchef-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt51fc44c10e0289c9\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs you can see, the “This program cannot be run in DOS mode” string appears after decoding, confirming our theory. Because a single byte XOR is one of the oldest tricks in the book, yara actually supports native detection with the \u003ca href=\"https://yara.readthedocs.io/en/stable/writingrules.html\"\u003e\u003cstrong\u003exor\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/a\u003e modifier:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003erule cobaltstrike_beacon_xor_strings\u003cbr /\u003e{\u003cbr /\u003emeta:\u003cbr /\u003e    author = \"Elastic\"\u003cbr /\u003e    description = \"Identifies XOR'd strings used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL.\"\u003cbr /\u003estrings:\u003cbr /\u003e    $a = \"%02d/%02d/%02d %02d:%02d:%02d\" xor(0x01-0xff)\u003cbr /\u003e    $b = \"Started service %s on %s\" xor(0x01-0xff)\u003cbr /\u003e    $c = \"%s as %s\\\\%s: %d\" xor(0x01-0xff)\u003cbr /\u003econdition:\u003cbr /\u003e    2 of them\u003cbr /\u003e}\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe can confirm detection for our yara rules thus far by providing a PID while scanning:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blte78d10de1484f20e/603e6ed5f9638443346d3da5/5-confirming-detection-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blte78d10de1484f20e\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eHowever, we are not quite done. After testing this signature on a sample with the latest version of Beacon (4.2 as of this writing), the obfuscation routine has been improved. The routine can be located by following the call stack as shown earlier. It now uses a 13-byte XOR key as shown in the following IDA Pro snippet:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt6ca50e04e513814b/603e6efbacf0d53d70c5accc/6-ida-pro-snippet-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt6ca50e04e513814b\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFortunately, Beacon’s obfuscate-and-sleep option only obfuscates strings and data, leaving the entire code section ripe for signaturing. There is the question of which function in the code section we should develop a signature for, but that is worth its own blog post. For now, we can just create a signature on the deobfuscation routine, which should work well:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003erule cobaltstrike_beacon_4_2_decrypt\u003cbr /\u003e{\u003cbr /\u003emeta:\u003cbr /\u003e    author = \"Elastic\"\u003cbr /\u003e    description = \"Identifies deobfuscation routine used in Cobalt Strike Beacon DLL version 4.2.\"\u003cbr /\u003estrings:\u003cbr /\u003e    $a_x64 = {4C 8B 53 08 45 8B 0A 45 8B 5A 04 4D 8D 52 08 45 85 C9 75 05 45 85 DB 74 33 45 3B CB 73 E6 49 8B F9 4C 8B 03}\u003cbr /\u003e    $a_x86 = {8B 46 04 8B 08 8B 50 04 83 C0 08 89 55 08 89 45 0C 85 C9 75 04 85 D2 74 23 3B CA 73 E6 8B 06 8D 3C 08 33 D2}\u003cbr /\u003econdition:\u003cbr /\u003e     any of them\u003cbr /\u003e}\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe can validate that we can detect Beacon even while it is in its stealthy sleep state (both 32- and 64-bit variants):\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://images.contentstack.io/v3/assets/bltefdd0b53724fa2ce/blt70a33ec8ee2d14c1/603e6eef7d801145b7fb6fd5/7-detection-beacon-blog-detecting-cobalt-strike.png\" data-sys-asset-uid=\"blt70a33ec8ee2d14c1\" alt=\"\" style=\"display: block;margin: auto;\"/\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo build this into a more robust detection, we could regularly scan all processes on the system (or entire enterprise). This could be done with the following powershell one-liner:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003epowershell -command \"Get-Process | ForEach-Object {c:\\yara64.exe my_rules.yar $_.ID}\"\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eWrapping up\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eSignature-based detection, while often looked down upon, is a valuable detection strategy — especially when we consider in-memory scanning. With only a handful of signatures, we can detect Cobalt Strike regardless of configuration or stealth features enabled with an effective false positive rate of zero.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eReference hashes\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cpre class=\"prettyprint\"\u003e7d2c09a06d731a56bca7af2f5d3badef53624f025d77ababe6a14be28540a17a\u003cbr /\u003e277c2a0a18d7dc04993b6dc7ce873a086ab267391a9acbbc4a140e9c4658372a\u003cbr /\u003eA0788b85266fedd64dab834cb605a31b81fd11a3439dc3a6370bb34e512220e2\u003cbr /\u003e2db56e74f43b1a826beff9b577933135791ee44d8e66fa111b9b2af32948235c\u003cbr /\u003e3d65d80b1eb8626cf327c046db0c20ba4ed1b588b8c2f1286bc09b8f4da204f2\u003cbr /\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003ch2\u003eLearn more about Elastic Security\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eFamiliarize yourself (if you haven't already) with the powerful protection, detection, and response capabilities of Elastic Agent.
